            leaderboard: googletag.sizeMapping()
                .addSize([320, 100], [[300, 50], [320, 50]])

                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/anie.202403585')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        //     .addService(googletag.pubads())

        //     .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-rail-1')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.rail)
            .setTargeting('Position', 'advert-rail-1')
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-rail-2')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.rail)
            .setTargeting('Position', 'advert-rail-2')

            googletag.pubads().disableInitialLoad();

        googletag.enableServices();

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="a026fe748ddb4e4a961de06305cf68fe">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '235955a5-3575-478a-9979-ee8511cc5a85'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d10749936" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>This work constructs a monolithic and amorphous electrode/electrolyte interphase at low temperature which is thin, stable, and contains more organic components, facilitating fast Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics, enabling sodium-ion batteries to achieve long-term cycling performance and great rate capability at −30 °C.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c9fe0400-5adb-4511-94c5-2f80101f38bb/anie202403585-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c9fe0400-5adb-4511-94c5-2f80101f38bb/anie202403585-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4b9c716f-f727-4599-b2a2-47bae8c1c007/anie202403585-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c9fe0400-5adb-4511-94c5-2f80101f38bb/anie202403585-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d10749941" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>In spite of the competitive performance at room temperature, the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is still hindered by sluggish electrochemical reaction kinetics and unstable electrode/electrolyte interphase under subzero environments. Herein, a low-concentration electrolyte, consisting of 0.5M NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolving in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether solvent, is proposed for SIBs working at low temperature. Such an electrolyte generates a thin, amorphous, and homogeneous cathode/electrolyte interphase at low temperature. The interphase is monolithic and rich in organic components, reducing the limitation of Na<sup>+</sup> migration through inorganic crystals, thereby facilitating the interfacial Na<sup>+</sup> dynamics at low temperature. Furthermore, it effectively blocks the unfavorable side reactions between active materials and electrolytes, improving the structural stability. Consequently, Na<sub>0.7</sub>Li<sub>0.03</sub>Mg<sub>0.03</sub>Ni<sub>0.27</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ti<sub>0.07</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na and hard carbon//Na cells deliver a high capacity retention of 90.8 % after 900 cycles at 1C, a capacity over 310 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under −30 °C, respectively, showing long-term cycling stability and great rate capability at low temperature.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202403585-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202403585-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the continuous development of renewable energy storage technologies and their expanding application, the demand for rechargeable batteries which can withstand extreme environments is increasing.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0001_R_d10749919e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Batteries are required to operate stably and safely at temperatures below −30 °C in aerospace, polar explorations, military equipment, deep sea, and some cold regions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0008_R_d10749919e766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as a prospective candidate for large-scale energy storage owing to their abundant natural reserves, ideal cost-effectiveness, and similar principle as lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0016_R_d10749919e769" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Notably, the lower first ionization energy (496 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) and smaller Stokes radius (4.6 Å) of Na<sup>+</sup> enables sodium-based batteries to exhibit stronger ion-diffusion ability and better active electrochemical reaction activity than LIBs in cold environments.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0012_R_d10749919e777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2d - Nat. Commun.">2d</a>, <a href="#anie202403585-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0027_R_d10749919e777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> Therefore, searching for low-temperature SIBs with satisfactory performance and long cycle life is imperative.</p>
         
         <p>Unfortunately, SIBs are still restricted by the slow electrochemical reaction kinetics, inevitably inducing severe performance degradation, fast capacity decay, and even safety issues with temperature drops.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0032_R_d10749919e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> The sluggish ion conductivity and migration ability through the electrode/electrolyte interphase are the major reasons for the deterioration under cold conditions. For example, the rapid decrease of conducting salt solubility of most carbonate-based electrolytes causes a significant reduction in conductivity when temperature drops, which results in dramatically increased concentration polarization, impedance, and bad electrochemical properties.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0040_R_d10749919e786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> On the other hand, the structure and composition of the solid electrolyte interfacial layer formed on the surface of the electrodes often show unpredictable changes at ultralow temperature, which might weaken the protective capability, limit the transport of Na<sup>+</sup>, and even hinder the normal operation of SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0034_R_d10749919e791" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5b - Chem. Eng. J.">5b</a>, <a href="#anie202403585-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0043_R_d10749919e791" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span> Hence, the development of electrolytes that have good compatibility with electrodes, which can construct a uniform, robust, stable electrode/electrolyte interphase with fast ionic kinetics at low temperature, is the critical need for further research on SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>A viable way to overcome such challenges is to formulate a satisfactory electrolyte owing characteristics of a low freezing point and high conductivity, and most importantly, having the ability to build stable, robust cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) and solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) with high ionic transport properties. Hence, ethers, which own superior compatibility with electrodes and low solidifying points, are considered to be promising choices as electrolyte solvents.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0044_R_d10749919e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> However, at temperatures far above the freezing point of the ether-based electrolyte, sodium salts will also precipitate and partially solidify due to the poor solubility of Na salt under cold ambient conditions, further hindering the development of low-temperature electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0050_R_d10749919e800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span> Currently, the idea of decreasing the salt concentration to prepare low-concentration electrolyte has been proposed and proved applicable in SIBs below 0 °C because of its low cost, fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics, and good wettability with separator.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0051_R_d10749919e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span> But research on the practicality of low-concentration electrolytes at lower temperatures, especially on the composition, changes and properties at low temperature of the electrode/electrolyte interphase is still lacking and ambiguous.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we employed a low-concentration ether electrolyte by dissolving 0.5M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DG) solvent for SIBs, aiming at constructing a stable interface layer with fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport diffusivity at low temperature. The results show that such low-concentration electrolyte effectively inhibits the salt precipitation at low temperature. More solvent molecules enter the solvation sheath with the decrease of temperature and concentration, resulting in more organic components involved in the formation of the interphase. Most importantly, a monolithic electrolyte/electrode interphase that is thinner, stabler, and contains more organic components, is generated during the electrochemical cycling at low temperature. Such highly homogeneous and amorphous interphase promotes the diffusion of sodium ions at low temperature due to its thin thickness, high organic content, and non-crystallized inorganic components, effectively reducing the kinetic limitation of Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the interphase and suppressing undesirable polarization under cold conditions. As a result, the inhibition of unfavorable side reactions in electrolytes and fast Na<sup>+</sup> interfacial dynamics are achieved, thus exhibiting both stable long-cycle capability and excellent rate property for Na<sub>0.7</sub>Li<sub>0.03</sub>Mg<sub>0.03</sub>Ni<sub>0.27</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>Ti<sub>0.07</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (LMNM‘T) cathodes (90.8 % capacity retention after 900 cycles at 1C, 72.6 % capacity retention at 5C) and hard carbon (HC) anodes (high capacity over 310 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) at −30 °C, respectively.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202403585-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202403585-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>To evaluate the low-temperature properties of low-concentration electrolyte 0.5M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DG (0.5M-D), 1M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DG (1M-D) electrolyte was employed as the control subject. Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the digital images of 0.5M-D and 1M-D stored at different temperatures, both of which can maintain the liquid state at −20 °C. However, when temperature decreases to −30 °C, partial solidification occurs in 1M-D, which may be attributed to the decrease in salt solubility, leading to precipitation at a temperature much higher than the freezing point.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0050_R_d10749919e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span> In contrast, no salt precipitation is observed in 0.5M-D, suggesting better suitability for applications in cold environments. Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0001">1b</a> exhibits the conductivity of the electrolytes at different temperatures. Although 1M-D has higher ionic conductivity at 25 °C (7.1 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>) while 0.5M-D only provides 4.0 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> due to its low salt concentration, the conductivity of 1M-D experiences a rapid decay when temperature drops. The ionic conductivity of 0.5M-D (1.7 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>, 42.5 % of that at 25 °C) is even several times higher than that of 1M-D (0.7 mS cm<sup>−1</sup>, 9.9 % of that at 25 °C) at −30 °C. Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0001">1c</a> shows the mean square displacement (MSD) plots as a function of time. The fitted calculations indicate that the self-diffusion coefficient of 0.5M-D (6.32×10<sup>−8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>) is significantly higher than 4.05×10<sup>−8</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> of 1M-D at −30 °C. High ionic conductivity and ion diffusion capability are the key factors for low-temperature SIBs, thus ensuring good electrochemical performance in subsequent tests. The oxidative stability of electrolytes was tested by linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) on Na//stainless steel cells. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0001">1d</a>, it can be identified that both electrolytes undergo severe decomposition after 4.2 V. Such cut-off voltage rises to about 5 V when the temperature decreases to −30 °C, which can be attributed to the low reactivity under cold condition. In addition to the obvious decomposition, the weak oxidation current at low potentials should also be considered, as it dominates the actual stability of the cells and the formation of the interphase.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0041_R_d10749919e888" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6a - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">6a</a></sup></span> Compared to 1M-D, 0.5M-D shows a smaller oxidation current before the cut-off voltage, and such gap becomes larger at low temperature. This indicates the reduction in concentration can inhibit the side reactions at low potentials and improve the oxidative stability. Thus, the results above indicate that the low-concentration 0.5M-D exhibits more satisfactory performance under cold conditions, and is more suitable for use in low-temperature SIBs.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202403585-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/baeb5ffc-74cb-4ee9-b742-0398c63e9f3d/anie202403585-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/baeb5ffc-74cb-4ee9-b742-0398c63e9f3d/anie202403585-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9d1e1a28-a807-41df-a33b-3d556d79a189/anie202403585-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/baeb5ffc-74cb-4ee9-b742-0398c63e9f3d/anie202403585-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403585-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403585" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Optical photographs of 0.5M-D (right) and 1M-D (left) electrolytes at different temperatures, and their ionic conductivity (b). (c) Fitted MSD plots of 0.5M-D and 1M-D at −30 °C. (d) LSV curves of 0.5M-D and 1M-D at 25 and −30 °C. (e) The fitted Raman spectra of 0.5M-D and 1M-D at 25 °C. (f) The representative solvation structures and the percentage of each solvation structure of 0.5M-D at 25 and −30 °C. (g) Na<sup>+</sup> RDFs and CN of 1M-D at 25 °C (g), 0.5M-D at 25 °C (h), and 0.5M-D at −30 °C (i).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The changes in solvation structure at different concentrations were explored by Raman spectra. As presented in Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0001">1e</a>, all Raman peaks locate near 741 cm<sup>−1</sup> (being assigned as A<sub>1g</sub> mode for P−F stretching in PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>), which are related to the bonding of Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0041_R_d10749919e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6a - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">6a</a>, <a href="#anie202403585-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0056_R_d10749919e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Electrochim. Acta">11</a></sup></span> 41.8 % of Na<sup>+</sup> in 1M-D are bound in the structure of solvent separated ion pairs (SSIP) where Na<sup>+</sup> is coordinated to DG molecules via oxygen atoms, while the remaining sodium ions exist in contact ion pairs (CIP) or aggregate (AGG) where PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> is coordinated to Na ions by F atoms. As the concentration decreases, red-shift can be observed in the Raman spectra, indicating a shift in the sodium ion binding interactions, with more Na<sup>+</sup> (51.6 %) tending to be present in the SSIP structure. This trend is favorable to faster transport of sodium ions in the electrolyte.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0057_R_d10749919e945" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Energy Lett.">12</a></sup></span> The solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrolytes was calculated by classic molecular dynamic (cMD). In the 1M-D electrolyte, 45.3 % of the sodium ions are bound in the SSIP structure and the remaining Na<sup>+</sup> are in the forms of CIP and AGG (Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0001">1f</a> and Figure S1). The proportion of SSIP in 0.5M-D increases to 52.6 % while the AGG decreases significantly, which is consistent with the results of Raman.</p>
         
         <p>To further analyze the changes in solvation structure at low temperature, the coordination environment details in 0.5M-D at −30 °C were also calculated. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0001">1f</a>, 34.2 % of Na<sup>+</sup> exist in the SSIP structure of [Na−DG<sub>2</sub>]<sup>+</sup>, the remaining 18.4 % are coordinated in [Na−DG<sub>3</sub>]<sup>+</sup> and the representative solvation structure of CIP is [Na−DG<sub>2</sub>]<sup>+</sup>[PF<sub>6</sub>]<sup>−</sup>. When the temperature drops to −30 °C, although the total content of SSIP decreases slightly, the solvation structure alters as more Na<sup>+</sup> tending to be bound in [Na−DG<sub>3</sub>]<sup>+</sup> structure, indicating more solvent molecules enter the solvation sheath. This trend was also confirmed by the radial distribution functions (RDFs) of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0001">1g</a>–<a href="#anie202403585-fig-0001">i</a>). The solvation structure is mainly formed by the coordination between Na<sup>+</sup> and O groups. The RDFs exhibit a maximum at 2.35 Å for Na<sup>+</sup>−O interactions, indicating that the sodium ion is surrounded by DG molecules as the first solvated sheath. The calculated coordination number (CN) of Na−O in 0.5M-D (2.18) is higher than that in 1M-D (1.99), and it further increases to 2.26 at −30 °C, which supports the conclusion that more DG enters the solvated sheath.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0058_R_d10749919e1000" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Phys. Chem. B">13</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To gain insight into the practicality of 0.5M-D in subzero environments, LMNM‘T//Na half cells were assembled to estimate the electrochemical performance, and the common electrolyte 1M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in propylene carbonate with 5 vol. % fluoroethylene carbonate (denoted as PC) was chosen as a comparison. The LMNM‘T cathode materials were prepared by simple solid-state reaction (as described in the experimental section at Supporting Information) and the phase was verified by X-ray diffraction (XRD) (Figure S2). Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">2a</a>–<a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">b</a> manifests the charge/discharge voltage curves with different electrolytes at a specific current of 0.1C (1C=173 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). When tested at 25 °C, both batteries with PC and 0.5M-D provide high discharge capacity (93.6 and 98.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) with a complete solid solution reaction. But at a sub-zero temperature of −20 °C, the cells with PC electrolyte deliver significant polarization which further boosts at −30 °C. Moreover, it can be observed that severe voltage drop occurs between 2.5 and 3.0 V during the discharge process, which may be related to the reduction of ion transport properties at low temperature.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0059_R_d10749919e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ChemElectroChem">14</a></sup></span> Whereas, there are no obvious changes in the curves of 0.5M-D except for little capacity attenuation. The advantages at cold conditions were further verified by cyclic voltammetry (CV) with a rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">2c</a>–<a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">d</a> and Figure S3–S4). The batteries applying 0.5M-D illustrate better reversibility, and it should be noted that the obvious change in the first cycle can be attributed to the initial activation cycle. The differences between the electrochemical behaviors of the two electrolytes become conspicuous when temperature drops. The redox peaks of cells with PC move rapidly to the edges, implying a serious polarization, and the peak area begins to decrease and some peaks are even illegible at −30 °C. On the contrary, the batteries assembled with 0.5M-D exhibit satisfactory electrochemical behavior, all redox peaks can be identified clearly and the curves overlap well in subsequent cycles.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202403585-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e16cfebe-045d-4a7d-aea0-b492694359c9/anie202403585-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e16cfebe-045d-4a7d-aea0-b492694359c9/anie202403585-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/96d8bb5c-1cd4-479c-b973-e9454309bcd0/anie202403585-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e16cfebe-045d-4a7d-aea0-b492694359c9/anie202403585-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403585-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403585" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The charge/discharge curves of LMNM‘T half cells with PC (a) and 0.5M-D (b) under different temperatures and the corresponding CV curves at 25 and −30 °C (c–d). Rate performance of LMNM‘T half cells with PC and 0.5M-D at 25 °C (e) and −30 °C (f). (g) The cycling performance of LMNM‘T half cells at 1C under −30 °C. (h) Cycling performance of HC//Na cells at 25 and −30 °C. (i) The first two charge/discharge curves of HC half cells assembled with PC and 0.5M-D at −30 °C. (j) The cycling performance and charge/discharge curves of LMNM‘T//HC full batteries at −30 °C.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Once operating temperature declines, SIBs tend to deteriorate and fail under high current density and long cycling. Thus, good rate capability and long cycle lifespan are essential for practical SIBs. As Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">2e</a>–<a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">f</a> and Figure S5 depicted, batteries with both types of electrolytes show great rate performance at 25 °C due to the fast intrinsic Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity of LMNM‘T.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0024_R_d10749919e1063" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3h - Adv. Energy Mater.">3h</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, when operating below 0 °C, the capacity of LMNM‘T half cells with PC degrade sharply at the rate over 1C at −20 °C, and it is almost impossible to cycle normally at 1C when temperature decreases to −30 °C. In contrast, batteries using 0.5M-D under −30 °C deliver high capacities of 88.2, 82.4, 77.0 and 66.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with retention of 96.4 %, 90.1 %, 84.2 % and 72.2 % at 1, 2, 3 and 5C, respectively. Their charge/discharge curves of LMNM‘T//Na at low temperatures are demonstrated in Figure S6–S7. More importantly, such low-concentration ether electrolyte has outstanding long-term cycling performance in subzero environments. At a current density of 1C, batteries with 0.5M-D present high reversible capacities of 89.6 and 91.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at −20 °C and −30 °C (Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">2g</a> and Figure S8–S9), manifesting optimum retention of 90.1 % and 90.8 % after 900 cycles (capacity decay rate about 0.01 % pre-cycle). The average coulombic efficiency (CE) in 0.5M-D are both close to 100 % at −20 °C and −30 °C. On the contrary, cells using PC only keep a capacity below 50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> which rapidly decays until it fails. Surprisingly, the cyclability of 0.5M-D at low temperature seems to be even better than that at 25 °C, which reinforces the potential of such low-concentration ether-based electrolyte for applications in extremely cold environments, making it an ideal candidate for low-temperature SIBs. The separators in batteries after 900 cycles were further analyzed. As the inset of Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">2g</a> shows, distinct yellow and black substances have been deposited on the separator cycled in PC, while no similar phenomenon occurs on that cycled in 0.5M-D. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to characterize such matters and the spectra indicate that obvious Ni and Mn signals were detected on the PC one while the separator of 0.5M-D almost devoid of the above elements. Also, more Na and F contents can be found on the PC-separator (Figure S10–S11). It can be inferred that 0.5M-D electrolyte is able to inhibit the dissolution of transition metals and the decomposition of electrodes, which is beneficial to keeping a long cycle life. Additionally, the morphology of cycled LMNM‘T was explored by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). Visible cracks were developed in different places on the materials cycled in PC, and all LMNM‘T in 0.5M-D maintained intact morphology (Figure S12–S14).</p>
         
         <p>Analogously, to understand the effect of 0.5M-D on the anode side, the cycling performance of HC//Na half cells was thoroughly researched under room and low temperatures at 0.2C (1C=330 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">2h</a>. HC//Na cells with 0.5M-D present stable cycling ability with a retention of 97.1 % after 100 cycles at 25 °C. And even at −30 °C, they deliver a capacity retention comparable to that of PC at ambient temperature. The voltage-capacity curves of PC and 0.5M-D at different temperatures are manifested in Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">2i</a> and Figure S15. The HC//Na employing 0.5M-D delivers a higher capacity of 330 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is mainly driven from the wider plateau region, corresponding to the co-intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> and solvent molecules. At −30 °C, almost all capacity is lost in the PC system. An opposite result was observed in 0.5M-D electrolyte group, where HC half cell still maintains a capacity over 310 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and the CV curves agree well with the analysis (Figure S16–S19). To better represent practical prospects, the LMNM‘T//HC full cells were assembled. Due to its low CE in initial cycles, the HC was pe-cycled for 4 cycles before matching. The voltage-capacity curves and cycling performance are illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0002">2j</a> and Figure S20–S21. High reversible discharge capacity about 92.5 and 86.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> were delivered at room and low temperature (−30 °C). Besides, the full cells show good cycling stability, with a high retention of 92 % after 100 cycles at 0.5C under 25 °C and 83.2 % after 100 cycles at 0.2C, −30 °C.</p>
         
         <p>To further evaluate the details of kinetics properties and interfacial behavior in different electrolyte systems, the surface (de)solvation process and charge transfer kinetics were analyzed. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed to reveal the interfacial properties by measuring impedances at different temperatures. The Nyquist plots of LMNM‘T half cells are displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0003">3a</a>–<a href="#anie202403585-fig-0003">b</a> with two electrolytes. All plots consist of a semicircle in the high-frequency region (attributed to the migration through the surface) and sloping lines in the low-frequency region (corresponding to the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the bulk). The equivalent circuit (Figure S22) was applied to fit the impedance spectra and the fitting results are listed in Table S1–S2. The charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) of PC-based cells boosts sharply with decreasing temperature, and even reaches an awful value over 117000 Ω at −30 °C, indicating a huge obstacle for the transportation of sodium ions. By comparison, the R<sub>ct</sub> of 0.5M-D electrolytes only increased slightly when the temperature dropped from 25 °C to −30 °C (from 78.8 to 147.9 Ω). The same EIS tests were implemented for HC half cells in PC and 0.5M-D systems, and similar results were obtained in surface resistance (R<sub>s</sub>) and R<sub>ct</sub> (Figure S23–S24 and Table S3–S4). Consequently, the low-concentration 0.5M-D shows satisfactory Na<sup>+</sup> transport properties at low temperature, improving the transfer kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup>, which brings about satisfied rate ability and high capacity. Then, the activation energy (E) of (de)solvation was analyzed by fitting the EIS according to the Arrhenius equation (Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0003">3c</a> and Figure S25). The solvation activation energy on the LMNM‘T cathode assembled with 0.5M-D is 9.28 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, much lower than that with PC electrolyte (55.89 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>). Lower desolvation energy of 0.5M-D (19.93 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) is also observed in the calculated results of HC while cells with PC deliver a high value of 55.89 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>. Apparently, 0.5M-D electrolyte effectively reduces the difficulty of the solvation/desolvation process on the surface, which is conducive to maintaining the rapid transport of Na<sup>+</sup> at low temperature. In addition, the activation energy E<sub>s</sub> of 0.5M-D also exhibited significant reduction, which may be attributed to the formation of the interphase with high ionic conductivity, which will be further discussed below.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202403585-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/876f8613-3674-4301-b42c-ecca5c42dd57/anie202403585-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/876f8613-3674-4301-b42c-ecca5c42dd57/anie202403585-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0aa132f8-893d-40e7-8016-a6add636c2e4/anie202403585-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/876f8613-3674-4301-b42c-ecca5c42dd57/anie202403585-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403585-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403585" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a–b) Nyquist plots of LMNM‘T//Na batteries employing two electrolytes at different temperatures and (c) corresponding fitted Arrhenius plots. GITT curves and the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of LMNM‘T//Na equipped with PC and 0.5M-D electrolytes at 25 °C (d) and −30 °C (e). (f) Cyclic voltammograms of LMNM‘T//Na with 0.5M-D at various sweep rates. (g) Corresponding linear fitting of the log (i) versus log (V) plots.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was also performed to evaluate the kinetics properties of PC and 0.5M-D under cold conditions. As Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0003">3d</a>–<a href="#anie202403585-fig-0003">e</a> illustrates, the overpotential is relatively smaller in 0.5M-D electrolyte during the major charge/discharge process, suggesting minimum polarization and better ion diffusion kinetics. Such advantages become more obvious when temperature decreases to −30 °C. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) was calculated based on the GITT curves. Although it is difficult to extract reliable quantitative value of D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>, its relative changes are still informative. The diffusion values in 0.5M-D are much higher and smoother than those in the PC electrolyte, especially in the discharge process, no matter at ambient temperature or −30 °C. Such outcomes illustrate that Na<sup>+</sup> are minimally resisted during transport and diffuses more efficiently. By an in-depth analysis of CV at various sweeping rates (Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0003">3f</a> and Figure S26), it is investigated that the rate capability is improved in 0.5M-D electrolyte. Compared to cells using 0.5M-D, the PC group shows a larger shift of the redox peaks and a more serious decrease in the capacity at higher sweep rates. Then, the average value of parameter b was determined to evaluate the step of Na storage in Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0003">3g</a>. The fitted b of 0.5M-D is much higher than that of PC electrolyte, hinting that sodium ion storage is more dominated by capacitive mechanism. Broadly speaking, the above results imply that 0.5M-D presents satisfactory Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, and can facilitate the improvement performance in ultralow temperature environment.</p>
         
         <p>The satisfactory adaptability of 0.5M-D to low-temperature environments is not only derived from good kinetic properties, but also manifested in the adjustment of composition and structure on the electrode surface responding to the change of temperature. To identify the interfacial chemical composition, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and XPS were utilized to analyze the pole pieces of LMNM‘T and HC. All poles have been cycled in 0.5M-D at different temperatures for 50 cycles. Owing to the decomposition of electrolyte during the cycling process, the surfaces of LMNM‘T and HC are coated with continuous CEI and SEI. Thin CEI and SEI (5.3 and 15.2 nm) were constructed on the electrode cycled at −30 °C, while much thicker layers of about 28.2 and 22.8 nm were formed at 25 °C, which can be clearly recognized in the TEM images (Figure S27–S30). To further analyze the structure, the CEI formed at different temperatures were observed at atomic scale by cryo-TEM without beam damage (Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0004">4a</a>–<a href="#anie202403585-fig-0004">b</a>). Interestingly, significant crystallization can be found on the CEI formed at 25 °C. The lattice fringes may be attributed to inorganic substances such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>O, etc. The fast Fourier transform (FFT) results also demonstrated the formation of polycrystalline components on 25 °C-CEI. In contrast, these inorganic compounds were not or poorly crystallized in the CEI under −30 °C, and were uniformly distributed in an amorphous form. Meantime, no obvious crystal information is observed in the FFT results. It is possible that the smaller thickness and lower temperature limit the nucleation and the growth of inorganic crystals.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0060_R_d10749919e1212" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a></sup></span> Previous research has pointed out that the transport property of Na<sup>+</sup> is strongly determined by the ionic conductivity of inorganic components such as NaF and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0055_R_d10749919e1221" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10d - Energy Storage Mater.">10d</a>, <a href="#anie202403585-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0061_R_d10749919e1221" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Mater. Lett.">16</a></sup></span> However, the crystalline inorganic compounds show unsatisfactory sodium ion transfer kinetics, leading to more difficult migration of Na-ion in them.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0060_R_d10749919e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a>, <a href="#anie202403585-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0062_R_d10749919e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> Tian et al.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0063_R_d10749919e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Mater.">18</a></sup></span> have reported that the ionic conductivity of Li<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> can be enhanced by amorphization. Similarly, the inorganic components formed at low temperatures as described above are also likely to possess higher ionic conductivity due to their amorphous feature. Apparently, such thinner, amorphous, and monolithic electrode/electrolyte interphase is favorable for rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion at subzero condition. This further explains the great kinetic properties of 0.5M-D at low temperature. The EIS results on the cycled LMNM‘T//Na half cells also support the conclusion (Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0004">4c</a>). The interphase impedance (R<sub>CEI</sub>) and R<sub>ct</sub> of the batteries cycled at 25 °C are 44.8 and 1335.0 Ω, significantly higher than that at −30 °C (17.7 and 335.3 Ω) (Table S5), the plots were fitted according to the model demonstrated in Figure S31.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202403585-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2ab3f568-046d-4f22-af43-285c0e37600d/anie202403585-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2ab3f568-046d-4f22-af43-285c0e37600d/anie202403585-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3fc7d5d0-6caa-43ee-a1ee-7cba4320ecd7/anie202403585-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2ab3f568-046d-4f22-af43-285c0e37600d/anie202403585-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403585-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403585" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a, b) The Cryo-TEM images of LMNM‘T cycled at 0.5M-D at 25 and −30 °C. (c) Nyquist plots of LMNM‘T//Na batteries using 0.5M-D after 50 cycles at 25 and −30 °C. The normalized XPS fitting spectra of C 1s, O 1s, F 1s, P 2p and Na 1s of LMNM‘T cathode (d) and HC anode (e) after cycling at 25 and −30 °C.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The chemical composition of such different interphase layers was studied by fitting detailed XPS peaks and binding energies. Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0004">4d</a>–<a href="#anie202403585-fig-0004">e</a> displays the normalized XPS fitting results of C 1s, O 1s, F 1s, P 2p and Na 1s of LMNM‘T after 50 cycles. When cycled at low temperature, there is a significant increase in C−O and C−H in C 1s, which are produced from the decomposition of solvents, while the peak representing Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> disappears. The same outcome is verified in O 1s spectra, the content of H−C−O enhances while the inorganic components Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and Na−O, which are generated by the combination of Na<sup>+</sup> with solvent decomposition products, reduces. In addition, a notable reduction in Na−F and P−O peaks originating from the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in F 1s and P 2p spectra can be observed on the low-temperature CEI. Based on the results above, it can be learned that the CEI formed at −30 °C consists of more organic matter and lower content of inorganic species than that at 25 °C. Overall, the composition of SEI changes less with the variation of temperature compared to CEI, but still manifests a similar trend. The formation of organic-rich CEI and SEI at low temperature confirms the change in solvation structure with more solvent molecules entering the solvation sheath, as revealed by classic molecular dynamic simulations and Raman results mentioned above, leading to more organic decomposition products from solvent. Generally, low-temperature SIBs tend to suffer from slow ion transport properties. On one hand, attributed to the elastic porous organic oligomers, higher organic content may favor the transport of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent while less inorganic composition may also benefit the transport of Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202403585-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202403585-bib-0053_R_d10749919e1293" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10b - ACS Energy Lett.">10b</a></sup></span> In addition, such amorphous interphase brings about higher ionic conductivity, as described above. Thus, the monolithic electrode/electrolyte interphase with more organic substances can provide favorable Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics, which explains why 0.5M-D helps SIBs maintain excellent performance at low temperature.</p>
         
         <p>The compositional distribution and chemical structure of CEI were further investigated by time of flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS). Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0005">5a</a>–<a href="#anie202403585-fig-0005">d</a> and Figure S32–S33 demonstrate the variation of main substance content with sputtering time at poles cycled at 25 °C and −30 °C. The intensity of organic compounds (CHOF<sup>−</sup>, CHNa<sup>−</sup>, CH<sup>−</sup>, C<sub>3</sub>HOF<sup>−</sup>, C<sub>2</sub>H<sup>−)</sup> in the amorphous CEI formed at −30 °C is much stronger than that at 25 °C, in line with the conclusions in XPS. Oppositely, the inorganic components (Na<sup>−</sup>, NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaOH<sup>−</sup>, etc.) in −30 °C-CEI are significantly less than the 25 °C one. Such conclusions are further supported by the 3D reconstruction images (Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0005">5e</a>). Meantime, the 3D images illustrate that the interphase generated at 25 °C exhibits a multilayer-type structure. In contrast, the CEI formed at the low temperature of −30 °C is more homogeneous from the surface to the interior, indicating a more uniform distribution at different depths. Therefore, it is this amorphous and homogeneous electrode/electrolyte interphase formed in 0.5M-D under subzero condition that brings excellent low-temperature electrochemical performance. As Figure <a href="#anie202403585-fig-0005">5f</a> illustrates, the optimum ionic conductivity of porous organic components facilitates the fast and efficient transport of sodium ions, while the inorganic substances in an amorphous state effectively reduce the limitation of Na<sup>+</sup> migration through crystals, thus realizing the fast interfacial dynamics at low temperature. As observed by TEM and XPS, such an interfacial layer inhibits the excessive decomposition and elemental dissolution of electrode, and is thin, uniform, and stable, which in turn benefits the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> across the interphase, further improving the performance of SIBs at low temperature.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202403585-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cdd05eb9-7bf2-4b4a-913e-612b5f2a07f3/anie202403585-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cdd05eb9-7bf2-4b4a-913e-612b5f2a07f3/anie202403585-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5448b9d1-4883-43b4-8971-e7ac5f22aaca/anie202403585-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cdd05eb9-7bf2-4b4a-913e-612b5f2a07f3/anie202403585-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202403585-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202403585" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a–d) The TOF-SIMS depth profiles of LMNM‘T cycled at 25 and −30 °C. (e) Corresponding 3D reconstruction images of NaOH<sup>−</sup>, Na<sup>−</sup>, C<sub>2</sub>H<sup>−</sup>, CHNa<sup>−</sup> and CH<sup>−</sup>. (f) Schematic illustration of the electrolyte/electrode interphase at different temperatures.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202403585-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202403585-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a stable, amorphous, and monolithic electrolyte/electrode interphase was constructed to achieve fast Na<sup>+</sup> interfacial kinetics at low temperature. This homogeneous interphase formed in subzero environments is thinner, contains more organic components with inorganic particles dispersed in the interphase in an amorphous state, which promises great ion transport ability, enabling SIBs to realize high performance at −30 °C. As a result, the assembled LMNM‘T//Na cells demonstrate remarkable electrochemical performance at −30 °C, including high discharge capacity of 91.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, superior rate capability (82.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2C), and excellent cycling ability (90.8 % capacity retention after 900 cycles at 1C). The HC//Na half cells provide high capacity over 310 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under −30 °C. Furthermore, the LMNM‘T//HC full cell also delivers a capacity of 86.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at −30 °C, corresponding to 93.3 % at room temperature. The proposed strategy provides new insights into tailoring interphase properties and electrolyte chemistry for realizing practical applications of SIBs under extreme environments.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
